Generalized Sequential Monte Carlo
Sampling for Redistricting Simulation
Philip O’Sullivan
Department of Statistics
Harvard University
Kosuke Imai∗
Department of Government and Department of Statistics
Harvard University
Cory McCartan
Department of Statistics
Pennsylvania State University
March 22, 2026
Abstract
Simulation methods have become important tools for quantifying partisan and racial bias in redistricting
plans. We generalize the Sequential Monte Carlo (SMC) algorithm of McCartan and Imai (2023), one of the
commonly used approaches. First, our generalized SMC (gSMC) algorithm can split oﬀregions of arbitrary
size, rather than a single district as in the original SMC framework, enabling the sampling of multi-member
districts. Second, the gSMC algorithm can operate over various sampling spaces, providing additional
computational ﬂexibility. Third, we derive optimal-variance incremental weights and show how to compute
them eﬃciently for each sampling space. Finally, we incorporate Markov chain Monte Carlo (MCMC) steps,
creating a hybrid gSMC–MCMC algorithm that can be used for large-scale redistricting applications. We
demonstrate the eﬀectiveness of the proposed methodology through analyses of the Irish Parliament, which
uses multi-member districts, and the Pennsylvania House of Representatives, which has more than 200
single-member districts.
Keywords
redistricting simulation • Sequential Monte Carlo • multi-member districts • gerrymandering
1
Introduction
Modern approaches to evaluating districting plans for racial and partisan bias increasingly rely on simulation-
based methods. Analysts use a simulation algorithm to sample large ensembles of alternative redistricting
plans from a target distribution that encodes relevant state and federal legal requirements, along with other
constraints of interest. By comparing an enacted plan to this ensemble, researchers can conduct statistical
hypothesis tests for detecting gerrymandering and quantify partisan and racial bias in the enacted plan. Such
simulation-based approaches can account for a state’s physical and political geography, as well as its jurisdiction-
speciﬁc redistricting rules. As a result, these methods have gained broad acceptance among scholars and courts
as a principled and empirically grounded framework for evaluating redistricting plans (e.g., Chen and Rodden,
2013; Magleby and Mosesson, 2018; Kenny et al., 2023; Becker et al., 2021; Herschlag et al., 2020; Chen and
Stephanopoulos, 2021; DeFord et al., 2022; Artés et al., 2025; League of Women Voters v. Commonwealth, 2018;
Rucho v. Common Cause, 2019; League of Women Voters v. Utah, 2024).
Markov chain Monte Carlo (MCMC) and Sequential Monte Carlo (SMC) methods are two widely used
approaches. MCMC algorithms begin with an existing plan and produce new samples by iteratively modifying it
∗To whom correspondence should be addressed. Email: imai@harvard.edu. Address: 1737 Cambridge Street, Cambridge, MA 02138.
We acknowledge partial support from the Sinnott-Wagner Geospatial Statistics Fund.
1
arXiv:2603.22188v1  [stat.AP]  23 Mar 2026
Generalized Sequential Monte Carlo Sampling for Redistricting Simulation
March 22, 2026
according to speciﬁed probabilistic transition rules (e.g., Mattingly and Vaughn, 2014; Wu et al., 2015; Chikina et al.,
2017; DeFord et al., 2021a; Carter et al., 2019; Fiﬁeld et al., 2020a; Cannon et al., 2022). In contrast, SMC algorithms
construct plans from scratch. Starting with an unassigned map, they iteratively and probabilistically carve oﬀ
districts in parallel, incorporating resampling steps, until complete districting plans are formed (McCartan and
Imai, 2023).
In this paper, we introduce a new sampling framework that generalizes the SMC algorithm of McCartan and
Imai (2023). The proposed generalized SMC (gSMC) algorithm oﬀers several improvements. First, we introduce
a new splitting procedure that enables the sampling of multi-member district plans, which are common in U.S.
local elections and many other countries. The gSMC framework can sample a region of varying size, which may
comprise one or more districts. In addition to expanding modeling ﬂexibility, this splitting procedure often
reduces computational cost. Empirical results demonstrate runtime reductions of approximately one-half to
two-thirds.
Second, gSMC can operate over multiple sampling spaces, including those based on spanning forests and
linking edges (Autry et al., 2023, 2021). Although the ultimate target distribution remains unchanged, these
alternative sampling spaces provide greater computational ﬂexibility. In these expanded spaces, plan splitting
can use arbitrary probability distributions over candidate splits at the cost of more complex weight calculations,
creating a computational trade-oﬀ. Empirically, particularly for large maps with many districts, these alternative
sampling spaces often reduce overall runtime.
Third, we derive minimum-variance importance weights for gSMC and provide eﬃcient algorithms for com-
puting them within each sampling space. The overall performance of the gSMC algorithm is largely determined
by its importance weights, which progressively reweight sampled plans to approximate the target distribution.
Our proposed weights achieve the smallest possible variance given the target distribution and chosen splitting
procedure, thereby improving the statistical and computational eﬃciency of the gSMC algorithm.
Finally, we incorporate a merge–split MCMC step, similar to those of DeFord et al. (2021a) and Carter
et al. (2019), into the gSMC algorithm, resulting in a hybrid gSMC–MCMC sampler. At each stage of gSMC,
two adjacent regions are randomly selected, merged, and then probabilistically re-split. While SMC samplers
constructs plans from scratch in parallel—often producing less dependent samples than MCMC samplers—they
can become ineﬃcient when there are many resampling steps, which reduces plan diversity, a problem known
as ancestor extinction. Interspersing merge–split MCMC steps within splitting steps mitigates this issue by
promoting greater diversity among partial plans throughout the gSMC’s iterative procedure.
We show empirically that the combination of these improvements drastically boost the performance and
scalability of the original SMC algorithm. Section 5 demonstrates the better performance of gSMC on a
validation example with the ground truth. Section 6 applies gSMC to two large real-world scenarios to which
the original SMC algorithm would not be applicable: Ireland’s Dáil Éireann, a multi-member map with 43
districts and 174 seats, and Pennsylvania’s State House, a large districting map with 203 single-member districts,
where the original SMC algorithm struggles to perform with any realistic sample size.
2
The Problem Formulation
This section ﬁrst formalizes the problem of redistricting simulation for single-member districts and then
generalizes the framework to multi-member district settings. Speciﬁcally, we characterize the target distribution
over redistricting plans from which we aim to draw a representative sample.
2.1
Setup
Suppose that we wish to sample redistricting plans consisting of 𝐷single-member districts. We represent the
map of a state as a graph 𝐺= (𝑉, 𝐸), where 𝑉= {𝑣1, . . . , 𝑣𝑚} denotes the set of geographic units partitioning
the state (e.g., precincts), and 𝐸is the set of edges connecting units that are legally adjacent. Each geographic
unit 𝑣𝑖has an associated population pop(𝑣𝑖), along with other relevant attributes such as racial composition and
electoral vote totals. For any subset of vertices 𝑉′ ⊆𝑉, its population is deﬁned as pop(𝑉′) = Í
𝑣∈𝑉′ pop(𝑣).

geographic units.
We now extend the preceding notation to the multi-member district setting. We introduce the more general
concept of a region, which is deﬁned as a tuple (𝐺𝑘, 𝑠𝑘) consisting of a connected subgraph 𝐺𝑘of 𝐺, induced
by a vertex set 𝑉𝑘⊂𝑉, together with an associated size 𝑠𝑘, a positive integer representing the number of
representatives elected from that region. For notational simplicity, we will often refer to a region simply by 𝐺𝑘,
suppressing the explicit notation for its size 𝑠𝑘. Any region with size 𝑠𝑘, which is greater than the number of
representatives a district is allowed to elect, is referred to as a multidistrict, since it contains multiple districts.
Figures 1b and 1c provide illustrative examples of regions and multidistricts.
Finally, we deﬁne a redistricting scheme, which speciﬁes the fundamental parameters governing the redistrict-
ing simulation, as a tuple (𝐺, 𝐷, 𝑆, [𝑑−, 𝑑+]), where 𝐺denotes the state map, 𝑆is the total number of seats to be
allocated, and 𝐷is the total number of districts, with each district’s size constrained to lie within the interval
[𝑑−, 𝑑+]. A redistricting plan is then given by 𝜉= {(𝐺𝑘, 𝑠𝑘)}𝐷

special case where 𝐷= 𝑆and 𝑑−= 𝑑+ = 1.
2.2
Target Distribution
Our goal is to sample redistricting plans from a ﬂexible class of target distributions deﬁned under a redistricting
scheme. In most applications, we seek plans in which district populations are comparable across districts. We
formalize this requirement through the notion of a balanced plan. Let [𝑃−, 𝑃+] denote per-seat population
bounds satisfying 𝑃−≤pop(𝑉)/𝑆≤𝑃+. A plan 𝜉is said to be balanced if, for every district, the population per
assigned seat lies within these bounds, i.e.,

for all 𝑘= 1, . . . , 𝐷.

deﬁne an associated target distribution 𝜋𝑟in an analogous manner,

3
The Proposed Algorithm
In this section, we introduce our generalized Sequential Monte Carlo (gSMC) algorithm, which extends and
improves upon the original SMC method of McCartan and Imai (2023). At a high level, gSMC generates
districting plans by initializing a collection of blank maps and then sequentially partitioning regions, including
those created in earlier splitting steps. At each splitting step 𝑟, the current collection of plans is reweighted
and resampled to target the intermediate distribution 𝜋𝑟. This “split-and-resample” procedure is repeated for
𝐷−1 rounds, progressively increasing the number of regions, until a ﬁnal collection of plans is obtained that is
approximately distributed according to the target distribution 𝜋𝐷= 𝜋.
The precise mechanics of how plans are split and which plans are selected for splitting depend on the choice
of sampling space, the splitting schedule, and the corresponding importance weights. We begin by presenting an

3.1
The Overview of the gSMC Algorithm
The gSMC algorithm belongs to the broader class of Monte Carlo methods known as Sequential Monte Carlo
(SMC) samplers (Del Moral et al., 2006). These methods are designed to generate samples from a sequence
of target distributions (𝜋1, . . . , 𝜋𝐷−1, 𝜋𝐷= 𝜋in our case) when direct sampling from the ﬁnal distribution is
infeasible. SMC samplers operate by constructing samples sequentially, using particles obtained at earlier stages
to guide and inform sampling at subsequent stages.
Algorithm 1 formally deﬁnes the gSMC algorithm, which is parameterized by a choice of redistricting scheme
and target distribution 𝜋, together with user-speciﬁed forward and backward Markov kernels, 𝑀𝑟(𝜉𝑟| 𝜉𝑟−1) and
𝐿𝑟−1(𝜉𝑟−1 | 𝜉𝑟), respectively. We let 𝛾𝑟(·) and 𝑍𝑟denote the unnormalized density and normalizing constants,
respectively, such that 𝜋𝑟(𝜉𝑟) = 𝛾𝑟(𝜉𝑟)/𝑍𝑟holds. The forward kernels encode the “splitting procedure,” which
speciﬁes how a plan 𝜉𝑟−1 with 𝑟−1 regions generates a plan 𝜉𝑟with 𝑟regions by partitioning one of its regions.
While one must be able to sample from each forward kernel 𝑀𝑟, the backward kernels are only used for the
computation of weights.

3.2
The Splitting Procedure
We now describe the splitting procedure at a high level, with details provided in Section A.2.1. To split a
partial plan 𝜉𝑟−1, we ﬁrst randomly select a multidistrict 𝐻ℓof size 𝑠ℓ, according to a pre-speciﬁed probability
distribution 𝜑(· | 𝜉𝑟−1) deﬁned over all multidistricts in 𝜉𝑟−1. We then sample a spanning tree 𝑇∗uniformly at
random on the selected subgraph 𝐻ℓusing Wilson’s algorithm (Wilson, 1996). We consider all possible splits
formed by removing an edge 𝑒∗from the sampled spanning tree 𝑇∗and assigning two new sizes 𝑠∗

The splitting algorithm has three inputs. First, we deﬁne a probability function 𝜑that selects a particular
multidistrict 𝐻ℓfrom the partial plan 𝜉𝑟−1. A natural choice is to set 𝜑(𝐻ℓ| 𝜉𝑟−1) ∝𝑠ℓ, so that larger
multidistricts are more likely to be selected for splitting (see Section 3.4.1 for details). Second, we deﬁne a splitting
schedule, which speciﬁes the allowable sizes of the two regions produced by splitting a multidistrict of size 𝑠at

For many problems, the marginal proposal density in Equation 5 does not have a closed form and cannot be
eﬃciently computed. Fortunately, for the gSMC algorithm, the marginal proposal density is readily available
due to the following two key facts. First, the term 𝜋𝑟−1(𝜉𝑟−1) is non-zero only if the partial plan 𝜉𝑟−1 is balanced.
Second, given that 𝜉𝑟−1 is balanced, 𝑀𝑟(𝜉𝑟| 𝜉𝑟−1) can only be non-zero if it is a plan with 𝑟−1 regions that
can be split into 𝜉𝑟. This means that 𝜉𝑟and 𝜉𝑟−1 share all but two regions in common, and thus 𝜉𝑟−1 must
be a plan formed by merging two adjacent regions in 𝜉𝑟while leaving all the other regions unchanged. It is
straightforward to show that the set of all such possible 𝜉𝑟−1 consists of plans made by taking a pair of adjacent
regions in 𝜉𝑟and merging them together. Therefore, Equation 5 is equal to a sum over all pairs of adjacent
regions in 𝜉𝑟where we take 𝜉𝑟−1 to be the plan made by merging the adjacent region pair.
Figure 3 illustrates the idea, where there are ﬁve pairs of adjacent districts in the plan 𝜉4 (bottom). For each
pair of adjacent districts, there is an associated plan 𝜉3 (top) formed by replacing the two adjacent districts with
the region created by merging them. The marginal proposal density for the plan here would be exactly the sum
over these ﬁve pairs of adjacent districts and the associated three-region plans formed by merging each pair of
districts. For an arbitrary plan 𝜉𝑟, this yields the following closed form expression of the optimal weights.

3.4
Practical Implementation Details
As shown in Proposition A.9, the mean of any summary statistics computed using the simulated plans converge
to their true mean under the target distribution as the number of simulated plans increases. In practice, however,
the quality of samples depends on the choice of a splitting schedule, the multidistrict selection probability
𝜑(· | ·), and K𝑟. Below, we brieﬂy discuss how these choices can be made in typical applications.
3.4.1
Choosing the multidistrict selection probability
The multidistrict selection probability 𝜑is used during the sampling stage to select a multidistrict to split and
for the weight calculations to compute the retroactive selection probability of a pair of merged regions. Any
choice that is a function of the region only is theoretically valid. We have found it computationally convenient
to either select a multidistrict uniformly at random or proportional to the size of the multidistrict.
3.4.2
Choosing a Splitting Schedule
We have implemented two types of splitting schedules: district-only and any-valid (this is only implemented
for single-member districting schemes). District-only splits is the same method as the one used in McCartan
and Imai (2023), where one of the split regions is always a district. In contrast, any-valid splits means that for
a multidistrict, we allow it to be split into two regions of any size greater than or equal to 𝑑−. In theory, the
splitting schedule could be chosen to allow for other types of splits. For example, one could split a map with 12
single-member districts into two regions of size 6, then each into regions of size 3, and so on.
However, implementing these schedules is computationally challenging in practice. This is because the
choice of splitting schedule changes the intermediate target distribution spaces and the weights for intermediate
steps (see Appendix Section C for details). Empirically, we ﬁnd that district-only splits yield weights with higher
variance at the intermediate steps than any valid splits. However, both appear to yield ﬁnal weights with similar
variances.
The choice of splitting schedule also aﬀects the overall computational cost. For a region (𝐻ℓ, 𝑠ℓ), Wilson’s
algorithm has a complexity on the order of 𝑂(𝑉(𝐻ℓ)2). Since generally larger size multidistricts have more
vertices, it is more costly to try to split multidistricts with a larger size. For this reason, we have found the
any-valid splits schedule to be less computationally costly compared to district-only splits. Overall, using
any-valid splits appears to result in a runtime that is roughly half to two-thirds of the runtime for district-only
splits.
3.4.3
Choosing K𝑟
The theoretical validity of the gSMC algorithm rests on the appropriate choice of K𝑟at each stage. In practice,
however, it is computationally infeasible to calculate the proper maximum for a given region. Computing a
global maximum over all possible regions in plans in the sample of size 𝑟is also intractable. In practice, therefore,
we follow the procedure suggested by McCartan and Imai (2023) and estimate the value of K𝑟before each step.
3.4.4
Diagnostics
The diagnostics play an essential role in implementing the gSMC algorithm. While diagnostics can never prove
the convergence of the algorithm in any particular application, it helps analysts detect convergence failure and
other potential issues. We follow the recommendation of McCartan and Imai (2023), in particular computing
the Gelman-Rubin ˆ𝑅statistic for various summary statistics based on multiple independent runs of the gSMC
algorithm (Gelman and Rubin, 1992; Vehtari et al., 2019).

4
Extensions
The gSMC algorithm presented in Section 3 can be extended in several ways: (1) the sampling can be done
based on diﬀerent sampling spaces with diﬀerent splitting procedures; (2) we can incorporate administrative
boundaries; and (3) MCMC steps can be added to boost performance. We brieﬂy discuss these extensions while
leaving the details to the appendix.
4.1
Different Sampling Spaces
Drawing on previous work (Autry et al., 2021, 2023), we can modify Algorithm 1, which operates on the space of
graph partitions, to sample plans using two diﬀerent sampling spaces: spanning forest and linking edge spaces.
While these sampling spaces ultimately lead to the same target distribution given in Equation 1, they can ﬂexibly
address the computational trade-oﬀs between splitting plans and computing weights. The two new sampling
spaces can be viewed as intermediaries in the sampling process for graph partitions. Spanning forest space
represents “stopping” the splitting process in Algorithm 2 after we remove the edge but before we remove the
trees. Linking edge space represents an even earlier step where we select a tree cut and then save both the trees
and edges. For both of these spaces, there is a many-to-one function of the graph partition they induce.
Figure 4 displays an example of a forest and linking edge space plan associated with an underlying graph
space plan. In contrast with Figure 4a, where we only store the graph partitions, as Figure 4b demonstrates, for
spanning forest space we save the trees we drew in the splitting process to create each region. Figure 4c shows
how for linking edge space we go even further, saving not just the trees we drew to create regions but also the
edge itself removed (highlighted in red).
The use of alternative sampling spaces allows one to select tree cuts according to an arbitrary distribution
over splits rather than selecting one of the top K splits uniformly at random. This makes the splitting stage
much less computationally costly because we can ensure whenever there is at least one balanced split it will
be chosen. This contrasts with the original splitting procedure where there is a greater chance of choosing a
non-balanced split even when balanced splits are present, requiring the entire procedure to be repeated. The
trade oﬀis that the computational complexity of the optimal weights is now greater than 𝑂(𝑉). Empirically,
we ﬁnd that in settings with large maps and many districts, a faster splitting procedure is worthwhile even if
computing weights takes more time. See Section A.3 for a detailed discussion.
4.2
Limiting the Splits of Administrative Boundaries
In many applications, analysts may also wish to discourage districts from crossing county, municipal, or other
administrative boundaries without just using those entities as the geographic units of the map. Rather than
encoding this preference solely through a constraint in 𝐽, we can eﬃciently incorporate it directly into the
sampling mechanism by drawing plans hierarchically with respect to the administrative boundaries. We do
this in the same manner as in McCartan and Imai (2023): spanning trees are drawn within units ﬁrst, and then
10
Generalized Sequential Monte Carlo Sampling for Redistricting Simulation
March 22, 2026
linked across units by drawing another spanning tree on the unit-level multigraph. These hierarchically sampled
plans are guaranteed to have no more than 𝐷−1 administrative unit splits (and a preference for even fewer
splits can be incorporated through the 𝐽function). However, the inclusion of hierarchical sampling requires
modiﬁcations to the backwards kernel, weights, and MCMC moves (discussed next). See Section A.6 for a formal
characterization of hierarchical plans and details of how the algorithm changes.
4.3
Incorporating MCMC Moves for Improving Convergence
Lastly, we improve the convergence of gSMC by incorporating Markov chain Monte Carlo (MCMC) moves
after each step of the algorithm. In the original algorithm, the forward kernel 𝑀𝑟always splits oﬀa new region.
Here, we allow for some 𝑀𝑟to sample a new partial plan while keeping the number of regions the same. So
long as we use a Markov kernel e
𝑀𝑟(·|·) that operates on the space of partial plans {𝜉𝑟} without modifying the
target distribution 𝜋𝑟, the convergence of the algorithm is not aﬀected, and in fact we can carry forward the
SMC weights from the previous iteration unchanged (Chenguang Dai and Whiteley, 2022).
To make this more precise, we ﬁrst note that for SMC samplers resampling need not be performed at every
step. Instead, if resampling is performed after 𝑚iterations of forward kernels to each particle, then we use the
product of the incremental weights from each splitting step for the resampling (Del Moral et al., 2006). Since
e
𝑀𝑟is a 𝜋𝑟-invariant Markov kernel, we can use the following time-reversal kernel as our backwards kernel 𝐿𝑟,𝑖,
e𝐿𝑟( ˜𝜉𝑟,𝑖| ˜𝜉𝑟,𝑖+1) = 𝜋𝑟( ˜𝜉𝑟,𝑖) e
𝑀𝑟( ˜𝜉𝑟,𝑖+1 | ˜𝜉𝑟,𝑖)
𝜋𝑟( ˜𝜉𝑟,𝑖+1)
.
Plugging these into the formula given in Equation 3 shows that the incremental weight is equal to one,
𝑤𝑟,𝑖+1( ˜𝜉𝑟,𝑖+1 | ˜𝜉𝑟,𝑖) = 𝛾𝑟( ˜𝜉𝑟,𝑖+1)𝐿𝑟,𝑖( ˜𝜉𝑟,𝑖| ˜𝜉𝑟,𝑖+1)
𝛾𝑟(𝜉𝑟,𝑖)𝑀𝑟,𝑖+1(𝜉𝑟,𝑖+1 | ˜𝜉𝑟,𝑖) =
𝛾𝑟( ˜𝜉𝑟,𝑖+1) 𝜋𝑟( ˜𝜉𝑖) e
𝑀𝑟( ˜𝜉𝑟,𝑖+1| ˜𝜉𝑟,𝑖)
𝜋𝑟( ˜𝜉𝑟,𝑖+1)
𝛾𝑟( ˜𝜉𝑟,𝑖) e
𝑀𝑟( ˜𝜉𝑟,𝑖+1 | ˜𝜉𝑟,𝑖)
= 1
Thus, MCMC steps do not aﬀect the weights at all.
We leverage this ability to add MCMC moves by incorporating a Metropolis-Hastings merge-split kernel
inspired by DeFord et al. (2021b), Autry et al. (2021) and Autry et al. (2023). The merge-split kernel, which can
operate on all three of the diﬀerent sampling spaces, works by selecting two adjacent regions in a given partial
plan 𝜉𝑟according to a probability distribution, merging and then splitting them as if they were a multidistrict
(i.e., drawing a tree using Wilson’s algorithm and then selecting a tree cut according to the rules of the sampling
space), and lastly implementing the Metropolis-Hastings rejection step. The formulas for transition probabilities
are presented in Section A.5. Critically, the merge-split kernel is 𝜋𝑟-invariant for all 𝑟.
Empirically, we ﬁnd that the merge-split MCMC steps substantially improves convergence. With typical
sample sizes (𝑁< 20 000), the SMC algorithm often struggles to handle more than about 25 districts at a time.
In contrast, for gSMC with MCMC steps, we have been able to eﬀectively analyze as many as 203 districts, as
we demonstrate in Section 6.2 in our application to redistricting the Pennsylvania state house.1
5
Validation
We now assess the empirical performance of the gSMC algorithm by comparing the sampled plans with the
ground truth in small-scale problems where it is possible to enumerate all plans (Fiﬁeld et al., 2020b). We also
examine how the diagnostics mentioned in Section 3.4.4 perform in practice. We perform validation in both
single-member and multi-member district settings. While we present the results only for single-member district
validation below, Section B.1 provides the results for the multi-member district validation.
1Note that the convergence performance degrades for the target distribution with 𝜌∉[.7, 1.3] and extreme constraints imposed
through 𝐽.

5.1
Setup
For single-member districting schemes, we adopt a validation setting similar to the one used in McCartan and
Imai (2023). The map is a 7-by-7 grid, where each grid square has the same population. There are a total of
158,753,814 exactly balanced 7-district plans on this map. The enumerations were carried out using the Julia code
from Schutzman (2019).
We use a target distribution with 𝜌= 1 and impose no additional constraints through the 𝐽term other
than exact population balance and contiguity. As noted in Section 2.2, values of 𝜌other than one may not be
computationally feasible for much larger real-world redistricting problems.
We evaluate both the gSMC algorithm with and without additional MCMC steps (Section 4.3) across
logarithmically spaced sample sizes ranging from 𝑁= 10 to 𝑁= 10, 000. For each MCMC round the expected
number of successful steps targeted was 7. The smaller sample sizes are not intended for practical use; rather,
they help illustrate the behavior of the method when the sample size is too small. At each value of 𝑁, we run
the algorithm independently 30 times so that bias, standard errors, and ˆ𝑅can be estimated with reasonable
precision.
To assess sampling accuracy, as in McCartan and Imai (2023), we evaluate the algorithms performance with
respect to rem(𝜉) · |E(𝐺)|, the number of edges that have to be removed to create a plan 𝜉. As discussed in
McCartan and Imai (2023), this value is strongly correlated with the target distribution when 𝜌= 1 and no
additional constraints are imposed through 𝐽.

of the estimates from the simulated plans shrinks and the estimates approximate the true distribution values
well, providing strong empirical support for the correctness of the gSMC algorithm.

Figure 6: The ˆ𝑅statistic, standard errors, bias, and RMSE for the median edges removed statistic, calculated
across 30 independent runs of the gSMC algorithms for the graph partition sampling spaces using any-valid splits

Figure 6 summarizes the results. As the number of samples increases, all of the expected indicators move in
the right direction: the ˆ𝑅statistics fall, standard errors decline, and RMSE decreases. For both variants of gSMC,
ˆ𝑅drops below the 1.05 threshold by 𝑆= 22, suggesting that the independent runs are producing consistent
answers and the remaining error is being driven largely by sampling variability, which decreases as the sample
size grows. When the sample size is small, the gSMC algorithm with MCMC steps tends to perform better than
the original SMC though their performance becomes indistinguishable as the sample size increases. The results
suggest that, as in McCartan and Imai (2023), the standard errors and ˆ𝑅statistics continue to perform well as
proxies for RMSE and convergence (and thus unbiasedness).
6
Empirical Applications
We apply the proposed gSMC algorithm to two real-world redistricting problems: drawing multi-member
districts for the Dáil Éireann in Ireland and drawing many single-member districts for the Pennsylvania House
of Representatives. These applications highlight the advantages of gSMC over the original SMC algorithm,
which cannot accommodate multi-member districts and does not scale eﬀectively to redistricting problems
involving hundreds of districts.
6.1
Multi-member redistricting: Ireland’s Dáil Éireann
The lower house of the Irish parliament is known as the Dáil Éireann and consists of 174 representatives elected
from 43 multi-member constituencies. Each constituency elects either three, four, or ﬁve representatives. One
policy question of interest is how districting plans would change under an alternative redistricting scheme, in
which each constituency instead elects four, ﬁve, or six members (Oireachtas Library & Research Service, 2023).
We conduct a simulation study to investigate the redistricting plans under such a scheme.
6.1.1
Setup
Ireland uses electoral divisions (EDs) as the smallest geographic units for redistricting. Although 3,441 EDs are
legally deﬁned, some have been amalgamated in publicly released data for privacy protection, resulting in 3,420
published EDs. We therefore use these published EDs as the units of analysis. When originally drawn in the
nineteenth century, EDs were designed to have roughly equal populations. Over time, however, population
sizes have diverged substantially. In the 2022 Census, the smallest ED had a population of 71, whereas the largest
had 43,905 residents.
13

Figure 7: Summary statistics for the sampled and enacted plans for Irish redistricting: (a) compactness (smaller
means more compact) and (c) county splits. Panel (b) shows the order statistics of the Polsby-Popper score by
district (i.e., within each plan, districts are ordered by Polsby-Popper score).
To account for Dublin’s distinct administrative and demographic characteristics, we partition the map into
two subregions—Dublin and the remainder of Ireland. We then sample districting plans separately within each
subregion and combine them for the ﬁnal analysis. This approach is consistent with the existing districting plan,
which does not contain any constituencies that cross the boundary between Dublin and the rest of Ireland. In
both subregions, we draw plans from the target distribution speciﬁed in Equation 1 with 𝜌= 1. We also impose
the same maximum population deviation as that of the current plan, i.e., dev(𝜉) ≤0.0812. For the non-Dublin
region, we incorporate the administrative boundary constraint described in Section 4.2 to limit the number of
county splits. For Dublin, we instead apply this constraint at the municipality level and impose an additional
soft constraint, where 𝐽(𝜉) is proportional to the number of splits, to further discourage municipality splits.
Under the alternative seat-size redistricting scheme (4–6 representatives per constituency), we reduce the
number of constituencies from 43 to 35 so that the average constituency size is approximately ﬁve. We generate
six independent runs of 10,000 samples each under both redistricting schemes. Sampling is conducted in the
linking-edge space described in Appendix Section A.3.2, using district-only splits and MCMC updates after each
SMC step. In general, we have found that the number of MCMC moves required for convergence depends
on both the size of the map and the number of districts. Thus, the number of MCMC iterations varies across
subregions and redistricting schemes: under the enacted scheme we used 10 and 100 expected successful MCMC
moves after each SMC step for Dublin and the remainder of Ireland respectively. For the alternative scheme we
used 10 and 20 respectively. The diagnostics discussed in Section 3.4.4 show no issues; all ˆ𝑅values for split and
compactness statistic are well under the threshold of 1.05.

6.1.2
Results
Figure 7(a) displays the fraction of edges removed for the enacted plan (red dashed line) and for the simulated
plans (histogram), where smaller values indicate greater compactness. According to this measure, the enacted
plan is more compact than 99.8% simulated plans. Panel (b) reports the order statistics of the district-level
Polsby–Popper scores, with the simulated order statistics shown as box plots and the enacted plan shown as red
solid circles. Thus, the rightmost box plot and circle on the 𝑥-axis corresponds to the least compact district in
each plan. Consistent with panel (a), the enacted plan appears generally more compact than the simulated plans
at the district level, though the simulations are as or more compact for the 5 least-compact districts. Overall,
there is substantial variation in compactness across districts. Lastly, panel (c) shows the number of county
splits for the enacted plan and the simulated plans. The enacted plan has fewer county splits than 86.0% of the
simulated plans.
Finally, Figure D5 presents the distribution of constituency sizes under both schemes. The red horizontal line
shows the counts of each constituency size under the enacted plan whereas a boxplot presents the corresponding
counts under the simulated plans. Interestingly, under the current 3-4-5 scheme the simulated plans tend to
have a greater number of constituencies with three or ﬁve seats as opposed to the enacted plans. Under the
alternative 4-5-6 scheme, constituencies with ﬁve seats are the most common.
These simulations demonstrate that it is possible to draw plans which achieve similar scores on traditional
redistricting metrics such as county splits and compactness while increasing the average district magnitude
(seats per district) and thus potentially increasing proportionality. Figure 8b demonstrates one such example
under the alternative 4-5-6 scheme.
6.2
Large-scale redistricting: Pennsylvania State House
Pennsylvania’s state House has 203 single-member districts, making it the second largest state house in the US.
This large-scale redistricting problem presents an excellent opportunity to examine the empirical performance
of the proposed gSMC algorithm. We examine the Pennsylvania state House map enacted in 2022 by the
Legislative Reapportionment Commission and how its partisan balance compares to simulated alternatives.
15
Generalized Sequential Monte Carlo Sampling for Redistricting Simulation
March 22, 2026
6.2.1
Setup
We sample maps from the target distribution given in Equation 1 while limiting the splits of Pennsylvania’s 67
counties as described in Section 4.2. We set 𝜌= 1 to sample compact districts, include an additional constraint
encouraging plans with fewer total county splits, and use a population deviation dev(𝜉) ≤.05, which is slightly
stricter than that of the enacted plan (5.2%). This population constraint translates to a tolerance of approximately
±3 200 people per district. Following the standard redistricting analysis, we use precincts as geographical units.
Pennsylvania has 9,178 precincts with a median population of 1,176.
We generate 16 independent runs of 10,000 samples each. Plans were sampled using the linking edge
sampling space described in Appendix Section A.3.2 and any-valid splits. A number of MCMC steps were taken
after each SMC step, chosen so that the expected number of successful MCMC steps would be 100. The overall
sample size and number of MCMC steps were chosen to ensure that all the diagnostics discussed in Section 3.4.4
indicated convergence. These include ˆ𝑅values for 9,623 summary statistics of potential interest, all of which are
below our recommended threshold of 1.05; 99.1% are below the stronger threshold of 1.01.

Figure 9: Boxplots of pseudo-logarithmically spaced ˆ𝑅values for the 9,623 summary statistics of interest for a
range of expected successful MCMC steps. Nine ˆ𝑅values less than 1 were truncated to 1. Whiskers are drawn
such that they span the full observed range.
We begin by examining how the performance of the gSMC algorithm improves by the inclusion of MCMC
steps. Figure 9 plots the ˆ𝑅values of the 9,623 statistics of interest against the number of expected successful
MCMC moves for several ﬁxed sample sizes (𝑁= 2 500, 5 000, and 10 000) using 16 independent runs. A greater
number of expected successful MCMC steps corresponds to performing more MCMC moves in between each
SMC step. The ﬁgure demonstrates that for a ﬁxed sample size, increasing the number of MCMC moves greatly
boosts convergence as measured by ˆ𝑅statistics. We ﬁnd that somewhere between 50 and 100 MCMC steps are
suﬃcient for obtaining satisfactory convergence results for these sample sizes.
Figure D6 of Appendix Section D.2 shows the fraction of edges removed from each plan. The enacted plan
has a smaller value than 99.4% of the simulated plans. Figure D7 shows the number of splits and total splits of
both counties and municipalities. In all four of the split statistics, the enacted plan is excellent. It splits fewer
municipalities than all the sampled plans and it has fewer county splits than 84.6% of the sampled plans and
fewer total county splits (the total number of contiguous county-intersect-district pieces) than 99.6% of the
sampled plans.
To examine the partisan bias of the enacted plan, we use an aggregation of precinct-level voting patterns.
The baseline partisan estimates are calculated by averaging the vote totals for several statewide elections based
on the data from the alarmdata package (McCartan et al., 2024).2 We caution that our simulation analysis is
2These elections are the 2016 and 2020 presidential elections, the 2016 and 2018 US Senate elections, the 2016 and 2020 state attorney
general elections, and the 2018 gubernatorial election.

Figure 10: Partisan statistics for the sampled and enacted plans. Panel (a) shows the expected number of
democratic seats for each plan. Panel (b) shows the order statistics of Democratic two-party vote share (i.e.,
within each plan districts are ordered by Democratic vote share) for ordered districts 90 through 140. Whiskers
are drawn to span the observed range of each statistic.
Panel (a) of Figure 10 presents the expected number of Democratic seats, which are calculated by counting
the number of districts in each (enacted or simulated) plan where the estimated Democratic vote share is greater
than 50%. We ﬁnd that, on average, the simulated plans yield about 102 Democratic seats compared to an
estimated 105 Democratic seats for the enacted plan. However, the 95% interval of the expected Democratic
seats ranges from 97 to 106 seats, and the enacted plan falls within that range (𝑝= 0.17).
To investigate partisan bias further, we plot in panel (b) the district-level order statistics of Democratic
vote share for ordered districts 90 through 140, analogously to the compactness order statistics in Figure 7(b)
(Figure D8 in Appendix Section D shows the full plot for all districts). When compared to the simulated maps, the
enacted plan delivers more Democratic vote share in some of the competitive districts. In particular, the enacted
plan has 25 seats with a Democratic vote share between 45% and 55%, compared to an average of approximately
43 in the simulated plans (𝑝< 0.0001). These results imply that the enacted plan favors Democratic party when
compared to simulated plans.
7
Conclusion
In this paper, we have shown that the new generalized Sequential Monte Carlo (gSMC) algorithm substantially
widens the applicability of simulation-based redistricting analysis. By allowing for new splitting schedules,
alternative sampling spaces, and the incorporation of MCMC moves, gSMC makes it possible to analyze
redistricting problems with multi-member districts and large-scale districting maps. Following our approach,
future work can incorporate recently proposed forward kernels (DeFord et al., 2025; Akitaya et al., 2026) into the
gSMC algorithm. Many empirical applications are now possible. For example, we are currently conducting a
comprehensive analysis of redistricting for state legislatures in the U.S. In addition, researchers can now analyze
redistricting problems in other countries that employ multi-member districts.

A

A.1
Definitions and Special Functions
We begin by ﬁrst formally deﬁning some general terms, special functions, and results used in the proceeding
sections.
Deﬁnition A.1 (General Notation and Terminology). We will adopt the following notation conventions
• 𝐷denotes the total number of districts
• 𝑆denotes the total number of seats in the entire state (in a single-member districting scheme this is equal
to 𝐷but for multimember districting 𝑆> 𝐷)
• 𝑁denotes the number of Monte Carlo samples
• 𝑟indexes the number of regions (which will be equivalent to time)
• 𝑘indexes speciﬁc regions or districts
• 𝑀𝑟denotes the forward kernel at time 𝑟
• 𝐿𝑟denotes the backward kernel at time 𝑟
Deﬁnition A.2. Let 𝐺= (𝑉, 𝐸) be a graph and let 𝐻1 = (𝑉1, 𝐸1), 𝐻2 = (𝑉2, 𝐸2) be subgraphs. We then deﬁne
C(𝐻1, 𝐻2) as the set of all edges where one vertex is in 𝐻1 and the other is in 𝐻2. In other words
C(𝐻1, 𝐻2) = {(𝑣1, 𝑣2) = 𝑒∈𝐸| 𝑣1 ∈𝐻1, 𝑣2 ∈𝐻2}
In essence, C(𝐻1, 𝐻2) counts the number of boundary edges between two subgraphs in the original graph
𝐺. It is important to note that the boundary edge set only depends on the vertices of the two subgraphs. That is,
if we have subgraphs 𝐻1, ˜𝐻1 where V(𝐻1) = V( ˜𝐻1) but ˜𝐻1 ≠𝐻1, then C(𝐻1, 𝐻2) = C( ˜𝐻1, 𝐻2) still holds.
Deﬁnition A.3 (Districting Scheme). We deﬁne a districting scheme (𝐺, 𝐷, 𝑆, [𝑑−, 𝑑+]) as the tuple consisting
of
• a map 𝐺= (𝑉, 𝐸)
• 𝐷total number of districts to be drawn on the map
• 𝑆total seats that the map will contain
• [𝑑−, 𝑑+] is a range of integer values for how many seats a district can contain.
gSMC is only guaranteed to work for [𝑑−, 𝑑+] where no value in the range can be written as the sum of two
other sizes in the range. In other words, for all 𝑠∈[𝑑−, 𝑑+] there exists no 𝑠1, 𝑠2 ∈[𝑑−, 𝑑+] such that 𝑠= 𝑠1 + 𝑠2.
Deﬁnition A.4 (Plan Operations). Let 𝜉𝑟and 𝜉𝑚be 𝑟and 𝑚region plans respectively (𝑟does not have to equal
𝑚). Then, we deﬁne the following operations
• Intersection - 𝜉𝑟∩𝜉𝑚
We deﬁne the intersection of the two partial plans 𝜉𝑟∩𝜉𝑚to be the set of regions in both plans ie
𝜉𝑟∩𝜉𝑚= {𝐺𝑘| 𝐺𝑘∈𝜉𝑟and 𝐺𝑘∈𝜉𝑚}
• Vertex Set enumeration - V(𝜉𝑟)
We deﬁne V(𝜉𝑟) to be the union of vertex sets of each region in 𝜉𝑟ie
V(𝜉𝑟) =

Deﬁnition A.7 (Splittable Score Function). A score function 𝐽is splittable if for any plan 𝜉𝑟with 𝑟≤𝐷regions,
there exists a sequence of plans 𝜉1, . . . , 𝜉𝑟−1 such that 𝜋1(𝜉1), . . . , 𝜋𝑟−1(𝜉𝑟−1) > 0.
That is, any hard constraints induced by the 𝐽function must make it possible to split a plan. In what
follows, we will assume that 𝐽is splittable unless stated otherwise. If the only hard constraints encoded by
𝐽are population balance and connectedness, 𝐽will be splittable. However, if additional hard constraints are
introduced, then some care is needed.

Deﬁnition A.9 (Region Tree). Given a map 𝐺, we deﬁne a region tree (𝑇, 𝑠𝑘) as the pair consisting of
• a spanning tree 𝑇⊂𝐺deﬁned on some subgraph 𝐻⊂𝐺
• a size 𝑠∈ℕ
Note we deﬁne the notions of being balanced and population deviation the same for a region tree as a
region.
Deﬁnition A.10 (Spanning Tree Set). Let 𝐻be a subgraph of 𝐺. We deﬁne T (𝐻) as the set of all spanning
trees that can be drawn on 𝐻so
T (𝐻) = {𝑇| 𝑇⊂𝐺is spanning tree and V(𝑇) = V(𝐻)},
where the size of spanning tree set is |T (𝐻)| = 𝜏(𝐻).
Deﬁnition A.11 (Induced Region Function). We deﬁne the induced region function R(·) as the function which
maps a region tree (𝑇𝑘, 𝑠𝑘) to a region (𝐺𝑘, 𝑠𝑘) by setting 𝐺𝑘equal to the subgraph induced by the vertices of 𝑇𝑘.
More formally, we deﬁne R(𝑇𝑘, 𝑠𝑘) = (𝐺𝑘, 𝑠𝑘) where G(V(𝑇𝑘)) = 𝐺𝑘.
The region function maps region trees to a region via the subgraph induced by the vertex set of the trees.
Therefore, for a region 𝐺𝑘, the only trees that could produce it are trees drawn on the vertex set of 𝐺𝑘. We
formally state this and provide its proof.
Lemma A.1 (Pre-Images of Induced Region Function). Let (𝐺𝑘, 𝑠𝑘) be a region on the map 𝐺. The pre-image of
(𝐺𝑘, 𝑠𝑘) under the induced region function R(·) from Deﬁnition A.11 is equal to
R−1(𝐺𝑘, 𝑠𝑘) = {(𝑇, 𝑠𝑘) | 𝑇∈T (𝐺𝑘)} = T (𝐺𝑘) × {𝑠𝑘},
which also implies
R−1(𝐺𝑘, 𝑠𝑘)
 = 𝜏(𝐺𝑘).
Proof. Let (𝑇, 𝑠𝑘) ∈R−1(𝐺𝑘, 𝑠𝑘). The deﬁnition of R(·) implies G(V(𝑇)) = 𝐺𝑘, which in turn implies V(𝑇) =
V(𝐺𝑘). Since 𝑇is a spanning tree with the same vertex set as 𝐺𝑘it must be a spanning tree on 𝐺𝑘and thus
(𝑇, 𝑠𝑘) ∈{(𝑇, 𝑠𝑘) | 𝑇∈T (𝐺𝑘)}.
Let (𝑇, 𝑠𝑘) ∈{(𝑇, 𝑠𝑘) | 𝑇∈T (𝐺𝑘)}. By the deﬁnition of being a spanning tree on 𝐺𝑘, V(𝑇) = V(𝐺𝑘). Now,
consider by deﬁnition of being a region we know 𝐺𝑘has the property that G(V(𝐺𝑘)) = 𝐺𝑘. Together, we have
R(𝑇, 𝑠𝑘) = (G(V(𝑇)), 𝑠) = (𝐺𝑘, 𝑠), which implies (𝑇, 𝑠𝑘) ∈R−1(𝐺𝑘, 𝑠𝑘).
□

Deﬁnition A.14 (Splitting Schedule). Given a ﬁxed number of seats 𝑆and districts 𝐷, a splitting schedule
S(·, ·, ·) is a function deﬁned as
S(𝑟, 𝑠, 𝜉𝑟) : [𝐷] × [𝑆] × [𝑆]𝑟→{(𝑠1, 𝑠2) ∈[𝑠−1]2 : 𝑠1 ≤𝑠2, 𝑠1 + 𝑠2 = 𝑠},
where 𝑟denotes the number of regions in the current partial plan, 𝑠is the size of the multidistrict being split,
[𝑛] := {1, . . . , 𝑛} for any 𝑛∈ℤ+, and [𝑛]𝑟denotes the Cartesian product of [𝑛]. Note that even though we write
𝜉𝑟in S(𝑟, 𝑠, 𝜉𝑟) that term is really only a function of the region sizes in 𝜉𝑟, however we write 𝜉𝑟for notational
convenience. A key requirement for the splitting schedule is that it must not yield sizes that would make it
impossible to continue sampling a full plan.
To see why the sizes of 𝜉𝑟term is necessary, consider the following multi-member scheme (𝐺, 𝐷= 5, 𝑆=
20, [𝑑−, 𝑑+] = {3, 4, 5}). Suppose that we have 𝑟= 3 and a plan 𝜉3 with region sizes (5, 5, 10). For this plan,
we cannot split 10 into (5, 5) as we would then be left with four districts of size 5, which is impossible to split
further. In contrast, suppose that we have ˜𝜉3 with region sizes (3, 7, 10). Under this scenario, we can split
10 into (5, 5) as we would be left with sizes (3, 7, 5, 5) and that can be split into (3, 3, 4, 5, 5). In other words,
(5, 5) ∉S(3, 10, 𝜉3) but (5, 5) ∈S(3, 10, ˜𝜉3).
Luckily for single-member districting schemes and multi-member district-only splitting schedules, the sizes
of the existing regions in 𝜉𝑟do not matter and the schedule is only a function of 𝑠. For notational convenience,
we will suppress any notation of 𝜉𝑟in the schedule going forward.
We also provide brief examples of splitting schedules. When sampling single-member district plans and
splitting oﬀone district at a time, the splitting schedule takes the form S(1, 10) = {(1, 9)}, S(2, 9) = {(1, 8)},
and so on. In contrast, if splits of any size are permitted, the splitting schedule would be S(1, 10) =
{(1, 9), (2, 8), (3, 7), . . .}, S(2, 9) = {(1, 8), (2, 7), (3, 6), . . .}, and so forth.
Remark A.1 (Splitting Schedules Eﬀect on Intermediate Target Distributions). While the splitting schedule has
no impact on the target distribution 𝜋= 𝜋𝐷, it has potentially signiﬁcant eﬀects on the space of intermediate
target distributions 𝜋1, . . . , 𝜋𝐷−1 by altering their support. These eﬀects manifest themselves in the intermediate
target distributions through the score function 𝐽, assigning probability zero to partial plans that are impossible
to create under that splitting schedule. Consider the district-only schedule, under which the intermediate target
distribution 𝜋𝑟assigns probability zero to plans that do not have 𝑟−1 districts. For example, Figure 1b would
have probability zero under the 𝜋2 of a district-only schedule because this splitting schedule does not allow
for having more than one multidistrict in a plan. In contrast, the any-valid schedule imposes the minimum
additional restrictions on the support of the intermediate target distributions.
23

A.2
Graph Space
We now present the results for both the forward kernel expression from Section 3.2 and the derivation of the
optimal weights for graph space plans from Section 3.3.
A.2.1
Graph Space Splitting
We describe the splitting procedure in more detail before presenting the necessary deﬁnitions, lemmas, and the
ﬁnal propositions. We ﬁrst specify a splitting schedule (see Deﬁnition A.14). Intuitively, the splitting schedule
speciﬁes the allowable sizes of the two regions produced by splitting a multidistrict of size 𝑠at stage 𝑟. For a given
SMC step, we choose a splitting parameter K𝑟∈ℕand apply Algorithm 2 to divide the selected multidistrict 𝐻ℓ
into two new regions.
Given a plan 𝜉𝑟−1, we ﬁrst select a multidistrict 𝐻ℓ∈𝜉𝑟−1 according to some distribution 𝜑(· | 𝜉𝑟−1), which
can be any probability distribution over the multidistricts in 𝜉𝑟−1. We then use Wilson’s algorithm to sample a
spanning tree 𝑇∗on 𝐻ℓuniformly at random. We then iterate over all possible tree cuts that can be formed on
𝑇∗according to the splitting schedule value S(𝑟−1, 𝑠ℓ) and sort them by maximum absolute pairwise deviation
(Deﬁnition A.13). We choose one of the K𝑟cuts with the smallest values uniformly at random and set our new
regions to be the two regions associated with the selected tree cut. The pseudo-code of this is given in Algorithm
2. We denote the sampling probability of this splitting procedure throughout as 𝑞(·).
Given this splitting procedure, we can express the forward transition kernel 𝑀𝑟(𝜉𝑟| 𝜉𝑟−1) in closed form as
the product of two components: the probability 𝜑of selecting a particular multidistrict 𝐻ℓfrom the partial
plan 𝜉𝑟−1, and the conditional probability 𝑞(𝐺𝑘, 𝐺𝑘′ | 𝐻ℓ) of splitting that multidistrict into two new regions
(𝐺𝑘, 𝑠𝑘) and (𝐺𝑘′, 𝑠𝑘′),
𝑀𝑟(𝜉𝑟| 𝜉𝑟−1) = 𝜑(𝐻ℓ| 𝜉𝑟−1) 𝑞(𝐺𝑘, 𝐺𝑘′ | 𝐻ℓ)
In Proposition A.1, we prove the following result. When 𝐺𝑘and 𝐺𝑘′ are balanced and K𝑟is chosen such that
it is greater than or equal to the number of balanced tree cuts that can be made on 𝐻ℓ, i.e.,
K𝑟≥
max
𝑇∈T(𝐻ℓ)
ok 𝑇, S𝑟(𝑠ℓ)
Then, we can derive a closed form expression for 𝑞(𝐺𝑘, 𝐺𝑘′ | 𝐻ℓ). This leads to the following closed form
expression for the forward kernel
𝑀𝑟(𝜉𝑟|𝜉𝑟−1) = 𝜑(𝐻ℓ| 𝜉𝑟−1) 1

and plans, i.e., K𝑟≥max𝜉𝑟−1∈P𝑟−1(𝐺) max𝐻ℓ∈𝜉𝑟−1 max𝑇∈T(𝐻ℓ)

be able to derive the closed form of our forward kernel for balanced plans. In practice, this is computationally
intractable to compute. However, we use the estimation strategy described in McCartan and Imai (2023).
Note that depending on the nature of 𝐺and the algorithm parameters, this splitting procedure can be very
ineﬃcient. Whenever we draw a tree 𝑇where the number of balanced tree cuts 𝑏= ok(𝑇, S(𝑟−1, 𝑠ℓ)) is smaller
than K𝑟, there is a K𝑟−𝑏

chance we will select an unbalanced split and thus be forced to run the procedure all
over again. This can be particularly ineﬃcient when there is large variance in the number of balanced tree cuts
between sampled trees.

A.2.2
Graph Space Optimal Weights
We must ﬁrst prove some supporting results that establish .
Deﬁnition A.22 (L-Region Ancestor).
Let 𝜉𝑟be an 𝑟-region plan. For a ﬁxed 0 < 𝐿< 𝑟we say an (𝑟−𝐿)-region plan 𝜉𝑟−𝐿is an 𝐿-region ancestor
of 𝜉𝑟if
• 𝜉𝑟−𝐿has 𝑟−𝐿regions or equivalently 𝜉𝑟has 𝐿more regions than 𝜉𝑟−𝐿.
• All but 𝐿of the regions in 𝜉𝑟−𝐿are also in 𝜉𝑟. In other words 𝑟−𝐿−1 of the regions in 𝜉𝑟−𝐿are also in 𝜉𝑟.
This deﬁnition captures the notion of a “path” from 1-region plans to 𝑁-region plans where a forward
kernel splits one region into two at each step.
Deﬁnition A.23 (L-Region Ancestor Set). Given an 𝑟-region plan 𝜉𝑟and 0 < 𝐿< 𝑟we deﬁne A𝐿(𝜉𝑟) to be the
set of all 𝐿-region ancestors of 𝜉𝑟.
Deﬁnition A.24 (New, Old, and Common Regions). Let 𝜉𝑟be an 𝑟-region plan. Let 𝜉𝑟−1 be a 1-region ancestor
so 𝜉𝑟−1 ∈A1(𝜉𝑟). Then we deﬁne the new regions of 𝜉𝑟relative to 𝜉𝑟−1 as the set of regions in 𝜉𝑟but not 𝜉𝑟−1.
We will denote this as NR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) and it is deﬁned as
NR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) = 𝜉𝑟\ 𝜉𝑟−1
We deﬁne the old region of 𝜉𝑟−1 relative to 𝜉𝑟as the region in 𝜉𝑟−1 but not 𝜉𝑟. We denote it as OR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) and
formally deﬁne it as
OR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) = 𝜉𝑟−1 \ 𝜉𝑟
Finally we deﬁne the common regions of 𝜉𝑟−1 relative to 𝜉𝑟as the regions in both 𝜉𝑟−1 and 𝜉𝑟. We denote it as
CR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) and formally deﬁne it as
CR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) = 𝜉𝑟−1 ∩𝜉𝑟
Remark A.2 (Region Ancestor Decomposition). Notice by deﬁnition for 𝜉𝑟−1 ∈A1(𝜉𝑟) we can decompose 𝜉𝑟
and 𝜉𝑟−1 as follows
𝜉𝑟= NR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) ⊔CR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟)
𝜉𝑟−1 = OR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) ⊔CR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟)
This will prove very useful later on.
Lemma A.4 (Old Region Characterization).
Let 𝑟> 1 and let 𝜉𝑟be an 𝑟-region plan. Let 𝜉𝑟−1 ∈A1(𝜉𝑟) be a 1-region ancestor. Further, let 𝐻ℓdenote the old
region and 𝐺𝑘, 𝐺𝑘′ denote the new regions, ie:
|OR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟)| = {𝐻ℓ}
|NR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟)| = {𝐺𝑘, 𝐺𝑘′}
Then the old region 𝐻ℓis equal to the subgraph induced by the union of the vertices in the two new regions 𝐺𝑘, 𝐺𝑘′.
In other words:
𝐻ℓ= G(𝑉𝑘⊔𝑉𝑘′)
29
Generalized Sequential Monte Carlo Sampling for Redistricting Simulation
March 22, 2026
Proof. Recall that we can decompose 𝜉𝑟−1 and 𝜉𝑟as follows:
𝜉𝑟= NR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) ⊔CR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟)
𝜉𝑟−1 = OR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) ⊔CR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟)
Since we know V(𝜉𝑟) = V(𝜉𝑟−1) this implies that the set of vertices in 𝐺𝑘and 𝐺𝑘′ is equal to the vertices in 𝐻ℓ.
To formally show this consider
V(𝜉𝑟) = V(𝜉𝑟−1)
V(NR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟)) ⊔V(CR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟)) = V(OR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟)) ⊔V(CR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟)) =⇒
V(NR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟)) = V(OR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟))
𝑉𝑘⊔𝑉𝑘′ = 𝑉ℓ
Since we know that 𝐻ℓ= G(𝑉ℓ) by deﬁnition then that tells us that 𝐻ℓ= G(𝑉𝑘⊔𝑉𝑘′)
□
This lemma formalizes the result that if a plan 𝜉𝑟only diﬀers from 𝜉𝑟−1 by one region, then this region in
𝜉𝑟−1 must be formed by the union of the vertices in the two new regions in 𝜉𝑟.
Corollary A.2 (Connectedness of New District and Remainder). Let 𝑟> 1. Let 𝜉𝑟be an 𝑟-region plan. Let
𝜉𝑟−1 ∈A1(𝜉𝑟) be a 1-region ancestor. Further, let 𝐻ℓdenote the old region and 𝐺𝑘, 𝐺𝑘′ denote the new regions. Then
𝐺𝑘and 𝐺𝑘′ share at least one edge in 𝐺, ie
|C(𝐺𝑘, 𝐺𝑘′)| > 0
Proof. Recall by Lemma A.4 above we know 𝐻ℓ= G(𝑉𝑘⊔𝑉𝑘′). Since 𝐻ℓis a valid region and thus connected that
implies 𝐺𝑘and 𝐺𝑘′ must share at least one edge in 𝐺, else G(𝑉𝑘⊔𝑉𝑘′) would not be a connected subgraph.
□
Proposition A.2 (Ancestor Set Equivalency). Fix 𝑟> 1 and let 𝜉𝑟be a 𝑟-region plan. Then the set of 1-region
ancestors A1(𝜉𝑟) is in bĳective correspondence with the set of adjacent regions in 𝜉𝑟i.e., AR(𝜉𝑟).
Speciﬁcally, the bĳection is the function that maps adjacent regions (𝐺𝑘, 𝐺𝑘′) ∈AR(𝜉𝑟) to the 𝑟−1-region
partial plan made by taking the other 𝑟−2 regions and a region formed by combining (𝐺𝑘, 𝐺𝑘′). So 𝜉𝑟\ {𝐺𝑘, 𝐺𝑘′}
and G(𝑉𝑘⊔𝑉𝑘′).
Proof. Deﬁne the following functions
𝑓: A1(𝜉𝑟) −→AR(𝜉𝑟)
𝑓(𝜉𝑟−1) = NR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟)
So 𝑓maps an 𝑛−1-region plan 𝜉𝑟−1 ∈A1(𝜉𝑟) to the two new regions in 𝜉𝑟relative to 𝜉𝑟−1 which we proved in
A.2 are adjacent in 𝐺. We will show that this function is a bĳection.
Part 1. 𝑓is injective
Let 𝜉𝑟−1, ˜𝜉𝑟−1 ∈A1(𝜉𝑟) such that 𝑓(𝜉𝑟−1) = 𝑓( ˜𝜉𝑟−1) = (𝐺𝑘, 𝐺𝑘′) (for some 𝐺𝑘, 𝐺𝑘′ ∈𝜉𝑟).
Consider that since 𝜉𝑟−1, ˜𝜉𝑟−1 ∈A1(𝜉𝑟) we know by A.2 that
𝜉𝑟= NR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) ⊔CR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) = NR( ˜𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) ⊔CR( ˜𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟)
𝜉𝑟−1 = OR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) ⊔CR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟)
˜𝜉𝑟−1 = OR( ˜𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) ⊔CR( ˜𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟)
By deﬁnition of 𝑓we have 𝑓(𝜉𝑟−1) = NR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) and 𝑓( ˜𝜉𝑟−1) = NR( ˜𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) so we see 𝑓(𝜉𝑟−1) = 𝑓( ˜𝜉𝑟−1)
implies
𝜉𝑟= NR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) ⊔CR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) = NR( ˜𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) ⊔CR( ˜𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) =⇒

𝑓(𝜉𝑟−1) ⊔CR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) = 𝑓( ˜𝜉𝑟−1) ⊔CR( ˜𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) =⇒
CR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) = CR( ˜𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟)
Now consider that by Lemma A.4 we know that
OR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) = V(NR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟))
OR( ˜𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) = V(NR( ˜𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟))
So we see 𝑓(𝜉𝑟−1) = 𝑓( ˜𝜉𝑟−1) implies OR(𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟) = OR( ˜𝜉𝑟−1, 𝜉𝑟)) as well. Since 𝜉𝑟−1 and ˜𝜉𝑟−1 can be decomposed
into their new and common regions with respect to 𝜉𝑟and we’ve just shown both of those are equal then we can
conclude ˜𝜉𝑟−1 = 𝜉𝑟−1.
Part 2. 𝑓is surjective
Let {𝐺𝑘, 𝐺𝑘′} ∈AR(𝜉𝑟). Deﬁne an 𝑟−1-region partial plan 𝜉𝑟−1 as the 𝑟−2 regions 𝜉𝑟\ {𝐺𝑘, 𝐺𝑘′} and a new
region G(𝑉𝑘∪𝑉𝑘′). This is clearly an 𝑟−1 region partial plan. Moreover since all but 1 of 𝜉𝑟−1’s regions are
shared with 𝜉𝑟so we see it is actually a 1-region ancestor. So by construction we have found 𝜉𝑟−1 ∈A1(𝜉𝑟) such
that 𝑓(𝜉𝑟−1) = {𝐺𝑘, 𝐺𝑘′} and thus 𝑓is surjective.
□

Remark A.3 (Eﬀect of Splitting Schedule on the Optimal Weights). Recall from Remark A.1 that the choice of
splitting schedule implicitly inﬂuences the space of intermediate distributions. This is reﬂected in the weights
as well meaning when the schedule is not any-valid splits then some of the terms in the sum may become zero
because the merged plan ˜𝜉𝑟−1 is not possible under that splitting schedule. The easiest example to see that is the
district-only schedule where unless 𝑟= 𝐷two merged districts will have probability zero in the sum as that
would create a plan with more than one multidistrict.
A.3
New Sampling Spaces
As mentioned in the paper, gSMC can be expanded to operate on new sampling spaces. While the ﬁnal output is
still plans distributed according to the same target distribution as before, these new sampling spaces allow for
trading oﬀsome of the computational cost of splitting plans in exchange for increased computational complexity
of computing the weights.

March 22, 2026
A.3.1
Spanning Forest Space
The ﬁrst sampling space the algorithm can be lifted to is the space of spanning forest plans which we will call
spanning forest space. Instead of a plan 𝜉𝑟consisting of a collection of 𝑟regions, we instead have a forest plan
𝐹𝑟consisting of a collection of 𝑟region trees 𝐹𝑟= {(𝑇𝑘, 𝑠𝑘)}𝑟
𝑘=1. Formally, we can deﬁne it as follows:

𝑟-region forest plan 𝐹𝑟as an unordered collection of 𝑟region trees 𝜉𝑟= {(𝑇𝑘, 𝑠𝑘)}𝑟
𝑘=1 such that the region sizes
form an integer partition of 𝑆, i.e. Í𝑟

the use of an arbitrary tree cut selection rule 𝑝cut(·|𝑇) and whereas before we didn’t save the tree and instead

only the edge removed. The signiﬁcance of this move is since we no longer need to integrate the probability of
splitting a tree over all possible trees that could be drawn on a region we can use 𝑝cut(·|𝑇) instead of the top
K method. 𝑝cut(·|𝑇, ) can be speciﬁc so that whenever there is at least one balanced tree cut it will always be
selected. This dramatically boosts the eﬃciency of splitting over the graph space forward kernel as we no longer
have to “waste” draws when the number of balanced cuts is less than K𝑟and we don’t select a balanced cut. We
formally present the splitting procedure and the derivation of its sampling probability below. We continue to
denote the sampling probability with 𝑞(·).

Corollary A.4 (Forest Space Forward Kernel). Let 𝜑(|) be a distribution over multidistrict trees in 𝐹𝑟−1 that
only depends on the induced plan 𝜉(𝐹𝑟). Let 𝐹𝑟−1 be a forest plan. If 𝐹𝑟is a forest plan such that there exists some
𝑇ℓ∈𝜉𝑟−1, 𝑇𝑘,𝑇𝑘′ ∈𝜉𝑟where G(𝑇ℓ) = G(𝑇𝑘) ∪G(𝑇𝑘′) then the forward kernel probability is
𝑀𝑟(𝐹𝑟| 𝐹𝑟−1) = 𝜑(𝐺ℓ| 𝜉𝑟−1) ·
1
𝜏(𝐺ℓ) · Eﬀ(𝑇𝑘,𝑇𝑘′)
Where (𝐺ℓ, 𝑠ℓ) = R(𝑇ℓ, 𝑠ℓ) and 𝜉𝑟−1 = 𝜉(𝐹𝑟−1)
Proof. We apply the same proof using the law of total probability from A.1, we just use Proposition A.4 instead.
For notational convenience we replace 𝜏(G(𝑇ℓ)) with 𝜏(𝐺ℓ) and write 𝜑as 𝜑(𝐺ℓ| 𝜉(𝐹𝑟−1)).
□
The optimal weights for forest plans look very similar to the weights in Equation 7 as well. The main
diﬀerence in their derivation is, unlike for graph space plans where there was a one-to-one correspondence
between adjacent regions and previous plans that could have been split to form the current plan, for forest plans
the function is many-to-one meaning for each pair of adjacent region trees 𝑇𝑘∼𝑇𝑘′ there are 𝜏(G(𝑇𝑘∪𝑇𝑘′))
1-region ancestors that share all trees but 𝑇𝑘,𝑇𝑘′. We will now make this more rigorous.
Deﬁnition A.31 (Forest Ancestry Deﬁnitions). For all the 𝐿-region ancestor (Deﬁnition A.22, Deﬁnition A.23)
and new, common, and old region deﬁnitions (Deﬁnition A.24) for graph space plans we deﬁne the analagous
forest space deﬁnitions to be the versions replacing regions with region trees.
Remark A.4 (Tree Region Ancestor Decomposition). Notice by deﬁnition for 𝐹𝑟−1 ∈A1(𝐹𝑟) we can still
decompose 𝐹𝑟and 𝐹𝑟−1 analagously to Remark A.2 as follows
𝐹𝑟= NR(𝐹𝑟−1, 𝐹𝑟) ⊔CR(𝐹𝑟−1, 𝐹𝑟)
𝐹𝑟−1 = OR(𝐹𝑟−1, 𝐹𝑟) ⊔CR(𝐹𝑟−1, 𝐹𝑟)
We now prove similar results to Lemma A.4 and Proposition A.2.
Lemma A.8 (Old Region Tree Characterization). Let 𝑟> 1 and let 𝐹𝑟be an 𝑟-region forest plan. Let 𝐹𝑟−1 ∈A1(𝐹𝑟)
be a 1-region tree ancestor. Further, let 𝑇ℓdenote the old region tree and 𝑇𝑘,𝑇𝑘′ denote the new region trees, ie:
|OR(𝐹𝑟−1, 𝐹𝑟)| = {𝑇ℓ}

Since we know V(𝐹𝑟) = V(𝐹𝑟−1) this implies that the set of vertices in 𝑇𝑘and 𝑇𝑘′ is equal to the vertices in 𝑇ℓ.
To formally show this consider
V(𝐹𝑟) = V(𝐹𝑟−1)

□
Notice the optimal weights are almost the same as in Equation 7 the only major diﬀerence is instead of
multiplying the weights by the graph theoretic boundary length between the two regions it is replaced by the
eﬀective region tree boundary length (Deﬁnition A.30), a sum over each edge on the boundary of the probability
that edge would have been removed to create the two region trees. The main eﬀect of this change is it increases
the computational complexity of the weights. Before for graph space sampling computing the weights could be
done in 𝑂(𝑉) time, requiring only a single pass through of the graph (ignoring the cost to compute any 𝐽terms).
Now the complexity is increased and it is hard to precisely characterize. For each pair of adjacent trees (which is
on the order of 𝑂(𝑟)) we must iterate over the boundary edges between them and compute the probability that
tree cut would have been chosen from the region tree made by taking 𝑇𝑘∪𝑒∪𝑇𝑘′. This requires a pass through
the entire merged tree 𝑇𝑘∪𝑒∪𝑇𝑘′ making it an 𝑂(𝑉(𝑇𝑘∪𝑇𝑘′)) operation. For a pair of adjacent region trees
the entire cost is 𝑂(𝑉(𝑇𝑘∪𝑇𝑘′) · C(𝑇𝑘,𝑇𝑘′)). Characterizing the complexity for a plan 𝐹𝑟is diﬃcult but it is
certainly more than the previous 𝑂(𝑉) cost to compute the weights. We have also observed empirically that the
weights become faster to compute as the number of regions increases.
Overall it is hard to precisely characterize the performance beneﬁt of sampling on this new space. Empirically
we have found that the acceptance rate for forest space sampling is essentially always higher than graph space
44
Generalized Sequential Monte Carlo Sampling for Redistricting Simulation
March 22, 2026
sampling on the same map, making the splitting step much faster as there are fewer calls to Wilson’s algorithm.
However, some of this performance gain is lost as computing the weights becomes costlier. Overall, we have
still observed that forest space sampling tends to be anywhere from 1.1 to 2 times as fast.
A.3.2
Linking Edge Space
The algorithm sampling space can be lifted even further to the space of spanning forests with a linking (or
marked) edge. We will refer to this space as linking edge space. For linking edge space we now keep track of
linked forests 𝐿𝑟= (𝐹𝑟, 𝐸𝑟) consisting of a spanning forest 𝐹𝑟and a set of 𝑟−1 edges 𝐸𝑟⊂𝐸such that 𝐸𝑟is a
spanning tree on the plan multigraph 𝐺/𝜉𝑟. The plan multigraph 𝐺/𝜉𝑟is the multigraph where each region is
a vertex and each edge is an edge between the two regions in 𝐺. Note this necessarily implies 𝐹𝑟∪𝐸𝑟forms
a spanning tree on 𝐺. We essentially obtain linking edge plan by stopping the splitting process even earlier.
Whereas before for forest plans we saved the trees after the split we now stop even earlier, saving the edge in
the tree cut as well. Now for each forest plan 𝐹𝑟we have many linking edge plans 𝐿𝑟associated with them. In
fact, the precise number of linked forests is exactly the number of spanning trees which can be drawn on the
plan multigraph 𝐺/𝜉(𝐹𝑟). We denote this number as 𝜏(𝐺/𝜉(𝐿𝑟)).
As with forest space, we deﬁne a modiﬁed target distribution 𝜋∗
𝑟to sample from which has a pushforward
target of 𝜋𝑟on graph plan space. The computational cost of splitting is exactly the same however the cost of
computing the optimal weights changes. The trade-oﬀis instead of computing Eﬀ(()𝑇𝑘,𝑇𝑘′), we only compute
the selection probability of a speciﬁc tree cut and the number of linking edges 𝜏(𝐺/𝜉(𝐿𝑟)), 𝜏(𝐺/𝜉(𝐿𝑟−1)) that
can be drawn on each plan. This makes the complexity of the weights on the order of 𝑂(𝑉+ 𝑟4) allowing for
diﬀerent trade-oﬀs compared to the forest space weights. A more detailed discussion is presented after the
proof of the optimal weights.
To split a linking edge plan the requirements are the same as with forest plans. All that is required is a tree
cut selection rule 𝑝cut(·|·). We then use the procedure in Algorithm A2. Notice this is the same as Algorithm A1
we just save the edge from the tree cut as well. We now formally present the modiﬁed target distribution and
derive the splitting probability and optimal weights.

Deﬁnition A.32 (Linking Edge Plan Deﬁnition). Given a districting scheme (𝐺, 𝐷, 𝑆, [𝑑−, 𝑑+]) and 𝑟≤𝐷,
we deﬁne a 𝑟-region linking edge plan 𝐿𝑟as the tuple 𝐿𝑟= (𝐹𝑟, 𝐸𝑟) where 𝐹𝑟is a 𝑟-region forest plan and
𝐸𝑟⊂𝐸is a linking edge set. We say that 𝐸𝑟is a linking edge set if 𝐹𝑟∪𝐸𝑟is a spanning tree on 𝐺(where
𝐹𝑟∪𝐸𝑟= 𝐸𝑟∪Ð

We now also deﬁne a new special function, the induced forest function.
Deﬁnition A.33 (Induced Forest Function). We deﬁne the induced forest function 𝐹(·) as the function that
takes a linking edge plan 𝐿𝑟= (𝐹𝑟, 𝐸𝑟) and maps it to the associated forest plan 𝐹𝑟. In other words,
𝐹(𝐿𝑟) = 𝐹𝑟

Notice for these weights there are two diﬀerences from the forest space weights in Equation 14. First, instead
of summing over all pairs of adjacent trees in 𝐹𝑟, we only sum over the 𝑟−1 pairs of trees linked by the linking
edges in 𝐸𝑟. Second, we only compute the tree cut selection probability 𝑝cut(𝑇𝑘,𝑇𝑘′|𝑇𝑘∪{𝑒} ∪𝑇𝑘′) once for each
linking edge and instead now compute the ratio
𝜏(𝐺/𝜉𝑟)
𝜏(𝐺/𝜉𝑟−1) as well. This changes the computational complexity as
the selection probability is now merely an 𝑂(𝑉(𝑇𝑘∪𝑇𝑘′)) operation but in return we must compute
𝜏(𝐺/𝜉𝑟)
𝜏(𝐺/𝜉𝑟−1) .
Thanks to Kirchoﬀ’s Matrix Tree Theorem, computing the number of spanning trees on a plan multigraph
requires computing the determinant of a submatrix of the multigraph laplacian. This is conservatively on the
order of the number of vertices in the graph minus 1, cubed. That means computing 𝜏(𝐺/𝜉𝑟) is an 𝑂((𝑟−1)3)
operation and computing 𝜏(𝐺/𝜉𝑟−1) is an 𝑂((𝑟−2)3) operation. Since there are 𝑟−1 linking edges we perform
the 𝑂((𝑟−2)3) operation (𝑟−1) times and the 𝑂((𝑟−1)3) operation once making the overall complexity
roughly 𝑂(𝑟4). Building the graph laplacian can be done with a single pass through of the graph making the
total complexity of the weights 𝑂(𝑉+ 𝑟4). In practice we can speed up this computation a bit by leveraging the
fact that the laplacian submatrix is a sparse, positive deﬁnite matrix and using computation methods designed
for faster performance on it. This means, in contrast with forest space, the weights become more expensive to
compute as the number of regions increases.
48
Generalized Sequential Monte Carlo Sampling for Redistricting Simulation
March 22, 2026

for some asymptotic variance 𝑉SMC(ℎ).
Proof. The gSMC algorithm is a partial rejection control SMC sampler where invalid particles are given the

A.5
Mergesplit Kernels
We now present the formulas for the proposal distributions of the mergesplit kernels used in the various sample
spaces. They are nearly identical to the analgous forward kernels shown in the main text except that 𝜑(· | ·) is
now a distribution over pairs of adjacent regions in a plan rather than a distribution over multidstricts in a plan.

A.6
Hierarchical Sampling
As mentioned earlier, gSMC can be modiﬁed to perform the same kind of hierarchical sampling as described in
McCartan and Imai (2023). This modiﬁcation also changes the target distribution and space, forward kernel,
optimal weights, and MCMC kernel. We will begin by assuming only one level of administrative hierarchy,
although as Section A.6.4 explains, this can be generalized to arbitrary levels of nested administrative hierarchies.
At a high level, this change is all driven by the use of a modiﬁed, hierarchical version of Wilson’s algorithm
which draws trees hierarchically with respect to the administrative units.
50
Generalized Sequential Monte Carlo Sampling for Redistricting Simulation
March 22, 2026
A.6.1
Modified Target Space
The hierarchical nature of the modiﬁed splitting procedure changes the space of plans being sampled from.
We deﬁne the plans produced by the procedure as hierarchical plans. For hierarchical plans their most useful
properties are sampled plans are now guaranteed to have no more than 𝐷−1 administrative unit splits and any
two regions will overlap in at most one administrative unit but the diﬀerences go even further than that. We
now formally deﬁne hierarchical plans, the modiﬁed target distribution, and several crucial related deﬁnitions.
Deﬁnition A.36 (Administrative Units). We formalize the notion of administrative boundaries by deﬁning a
set of administrative units denoted by 𝐴and an associated map 𝜂: 𝑉→𝐴which maps vertices in 𝐺to their
associated administrative unit. This function induces an equivalence relation ∼𝜂on vertices where 𝑣∼𝜂𝑢for
nodes 𝑣and 𝑢if and only if 𝜂(𝑣) = 𝜂(𝑢). We often use a simplifying notation 𝜂−1(𝑎) to denote the subgraph
induced by the vertices 𝑣∈𝑉where 𝜂(𝑣) = 𝑎.
Deﬁnition A.37 (Administrative Splits). Given a map 𝐺with a set of administrative units (𝜂, 𝐴) and a 𝑟-region
plan 𝜉𝑟we deﬁne the number of administrative splits of the plan as the sum of the number of connected
components of each region intersect administrative unit minus the number of units:
spl(𝜉𝑟) =
 ∑︁
𝑎∈𝐴
𝑟∑︁
𝑘=1
𝐶(𝜂−1(𝑎) ∩𝐺𝑘)

Where 𝐶(·) counts the number of connected components in the subgraph 𝜂−1(𝑎) ∩𝐺𝑘.
Deﬁnition A.38 (Hierarchically Connected). Given a set of administrative units (𝜂, 𝐴) we say a region (𝐺𝑘, 𝑠𝑘)
is hierarchically connected if for every administrative unit 𝑎∈𝐴that unit intersected with the region has at
most one connected component. In other words:
∀𝑎∈𝐴

We say a plan 𝜉𝑟is hierarchically connected if every region is hierarchically connected.
Deﬁnition A.39 (Administrative Quotient Graph). Suppose we have a map 𝐺with a set of administrative units
(𝜂, 𝐴). Let 𝐻⊂𝐺be a subgraph of 𝐺. We deﬁne the administrative level quotient multigraph 𝐻/∼𝜂as the
multigraph produced by quotienting by 𝜂. In other words, this is the multigraph where each vertex is a unit
𝑎∈𝐴such that 𝐻∩𝜂−1(𝑎) ≠∅and for 𝑎, 𝑏∈𝐴each edge in the multigraph corresponds to an edge between
two vertices in 𝐻across 𝑎, 𝑏.

∀𝑎∈𝐴=⇒𝑇𝜂∩𝜂−1(𝑎) is still a spanning tree

Deﬁnition A.42 (Hierarchical Plan Tree).
Given a plan 𝜉𝑟we say that a spanning tree 𝑇𝜂on 𝐺is a (𝜉𝑟, 𝜂)-hierarchical plan tree (or just a hierarchical
plan tree for short) if it satisﬁes the following two properties
1. 𝑇𝜂is an 𝜂-hierarchical tree on 𝐺
2. For each region 𝐺𝑘∈𝜉𝑟the restriction 𝑇𝜂∩𝐺𝑘is an 𝜂-hierarchical tree on 𝐺𝑘.
Essentially a hierarchical plan tree is a hierarchical tree on the entire map such that the restriction to any
region is also a hierarchical tree on that region. This type of tree is useful as we will prove later that the existence
of a hierarchical plan tree is equivalent to proving a plan is hierarchically splittable.
Deﬁnition A.43 (Hierarchical Plan). We say a plan 𝜉𝑟is an 𝜂-hierarchical plan (or simply a hierarchical plan) if
it is possible to draw a plan hierarchical tree on it.
We can now deﬁne the modiﬁed hierarchical target distribution which is designed to sample hierarchical
plans on 𝐺, (𝐴, 𝜂) where compactness is now parameterized by 𝜏𝜂(·), not 𝜏(·).
Deﬁnition A.44 (Hierarchical Target Distribution). The modiﬁed hierarchical version of gSMC is designed to
sample plans from the following class of target distributions:

We now present an equivalent characterization of hierarchical plans in Proposition A.13. But ﬁrst we must
deﬁne two new concepts.
Deﬁnition A.45 (Administratively Adjacent). We say two regions 𝐺𝑘, 𝐺𝑘′ are administratively adjacent if there
exists at least one administrative unit 𝑧∈𝐴such that 𝐺𝑘, 𝐺𝑘′ are adjacent within 𝑧. In other words if
∃𝑧∈𝐴C(𝐺𝑘∩𝜂−1(𝑧), 𝐺𝑘′ ∩𝜂−1(𝑧)) ≠∅
52
Generalized Sequential Monte Carlo Sampling for Redistricting Simulation
March 22, 2026
Deﬁnition A.46 (Administratively Adjacent Quotient Graph). For a plan 𝜉𝑟, we deﬁne the administrative
region quotient graph 𝐺/(∼𝜂𝜉𝑟) to be a subgraph of 𝐺/𝜉𝑟where each vertex is a region and we only count an
edge (𝑢, 𝑣) = 𝑒if 𝑢and 𝑣are in the same administrative unit (𝜂(𝑢) = 𝜂(𝑣)) but diﬀerent regions.
This is essentially the plan quotient multigraph but instead of counting all edges between regions we
only count edges between regions that are in the same administrative unit. In other words, 𝐺/(∼𝜂𝜉𝑟) is the
administrative adjacency graph between regions in 𝜉𝑟.
We will show that a plan 𝜉𝑟is hierarchical if and only if it is both administratively connected and for each
connected component of 𝐺/(∼𝜂𝜉𝑟) the number of splits is equal to the number of regions in the component
minus 1. We present this formally in Proposition A.13 but we ﬁrst introduce some supporting results.
Lemma A.12 (Joining Disjoint Trees at a Vertex). Let ˜𝐺be an undirected multigraph and let 𝑇1,𝑇2 be spanning
trees on the subgraphs 𝐻1, 𝐻2 ⊂˜𝐺where 𝐻1, 𝐻2 have 𝑛1, 𝑛2 vertices respectively . Suppose that 𝑇1 ∩𝑇2 = ({𝑣}, ∅).
Then 𝑇= 𝑇1 ∪𝑇2 is also a spanning tree on 𝐻1 ∪𝐻2.
Proof. To show 𝑇is a tree we just need to show its connected and has |V(𝑇)| −1 edges
Part 1. 𝑇is connected
To show 𝑇is connected pick an arbitrary 𝑢∈𝑇and WLOG suppose 𝑢∈𝑇1. Since 𝑇1 is connected starting from
𝑢we can visit every vertex in 𝑇1. This includes 𝑣. Since we can visit 𝑣and 𝑣∈𝑇2 then since 𝑇2 is connected we
can also visit every vertex in 𝑇2, therefore 𝑇is connected.
Part 2. 𝑇has |V(𝑇)| −1 edges
Note that since 𝑇1 ∩𝑇2 = ({𝑣}, ∅) then we know E(𝑇) = E(𝑇1) ⊔E(𝑇2) and since 𝑇1 and 𝑇2 are trees we know
|E(𝑇)| = 𝑛1 −1 + 𝑛2 −1.
Now consider that since 𝑇1,𝑇2 only share one vertex 𝑣then we know the vertex set of 𝑇are equal to the vertices
in 𝑇1 and 𝑇2 without 𝑣and 𝑣so
V(𝑇) = (V(𝑇1) \ {𝑣}) ∪{𝑣} ∪(V(𝑇2) \ {𝑣}) =⇒
|V(𝑇)| = |(V(𝑇1) \ {𝑣})| + |{𝑣}| + |(V(𝑇2) \ {𝑣})|
|V(𝑇)| = 𝑛1 −1 + 1 + 𝑛2 −1
|V(𝑇)| = 𝑛1 + 𝑛2 −1
Thus we’ve shown that 𝑇has 𝑛1 + 𝑛2 −2 edges and 𝑛1 + 𝑛2 −1 vertices. Therefore by deﬁnition of spanning
trees we know 𝑇is a spanning tree on 𝐻1 ∪𝐻2.
□
Lemma A.13 (Joining Spanning Trees). Let 𝐺𝑎, 𝐺𝑏be disjoint regions of 𝐺. Now let 𝑇1 be a spanning tree on 𝐻1
and 𝑇2 be a spanning tree on 𝐻2. Then for any 𝑒∈C(𝑇1,𝑇2) the subgraph 𝑇= 𝑇1 ∪{𝑒} ∪𝑇2 is a spanning tree on
the merged region 𝐺𝐿= 𝐺𝑎∪𝐺𝑏.
Proof. Let 𝑛𝑎= |V(𝐺𝑎)| and 𝑛𝑏= |V(𝐺𝑏)|. Since 𝑇1,𝑇2 are spanning trees on those regions we know V(𝐺𝑎) =
V(𝑇1) and V(𝐺𝑏) = V(𝑇2) and thus by deﬁnition of being a spanning tree we know that 𝑇1 has 𝑛𝑎−1 edges
and 𝑇2 has 𝑛𝑏−1 edges.
Now let 𝑒∈C(𝑇1,𝑇2) and consider 𝑇= 𝑇1 ∪{𝑒} ∪𝑇2. Since 𝑇1 and 𝑇2 are connected then we see that 𝑇must
also be connected as if we pick an arbitrary 𝑣∈𝑇1 since 𝑇1 is connected we can visit every vertex there. Further,
since 𝑒∈C(𝑇1,𝑇2) we can use 𝑒to traverse from 𝑇1 to 𝑇2 and then since 𝑇2 is connected we can traverse the
rest of 𝑇2.
Now consider since 𝑇1 and 𝑇2 have disjoint vertex sets then we know 𝑒∉𝑇1,𝑇2. Therefore we know
|E(𝑇)| = |E(𝑇1)| + |{𝑒}| + |E(𝑇2)|
= 𝑛𝑎−1 + 1 + 𝑛𝑏−1
53
Generalized Sequential Monte Carlo Sampling for Redistricting Simulation
March 22, 2026
= 𝑛𝑎+ 𝑛𝑏−1
Thus since 𝑇is connected and has 𝑛𝑎+ 𝑛𝑏−1 vertices then by deﬁnition of spanning trees 𝑇is a spanning tree
on 𝐺𝑎∪𝐺𝑏.
□

Lemma A.14 (Drawing Hierarchical Tree on Administratively Adjacent Connected Component).
Let 𝜉𝑟be a hierarchically connected plan. Now, let 𝒞𝑖be a connected component of the administratively adjacent
quotient graph 𝐺/(∼𝜂𝜉𝑟). If the number of splits in 𝒞𝑖is equal to the number of regions in the component minus one,
ie spl(𝒞𝑖) = |V(𝒞𝑖)| −1, then it is possible to draw a tree 𝑇𝜂
𝑖such that
• 𝑇𝜂
𝑖restricted to any region 𝐺𝑘∈𝒞𝑖is a 𝜂-hierarchical tree
• 𝑇𝜂

Ð
𝐺𝑘∈𝒞𝑖𝐺𝑘
